    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="23669b51808b4d90851582847ba95a80">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd198e467-b7a8-4dfd-93a0-7776fd31e939'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d167095018" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries are attracting great attention as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries due to the lower cost and better sustainability of sodium. Although the metal sulfide-based anodes demonstrate much higher theoretical capacity than the hard carbon anodes, the severe capacity degradation and inferior rate capability caused by poor electrical conductivity and sluggish kinetics hinder their applications. Herein, a novel bimetallic sulfide-based anode wrapped by reduced graphene oxide (i.e., Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO) is presented, in which the heterointerfaces between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> are well distributed among the composite, leading to the promoted charge transfer and the improved Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. Combined with electrolyte optimization, the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO demonstrates excellent electrochemical performance, including excellent rate capabilities over 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a long lifespan over 1000 cycles. This work indicates the significance of the synergistic effect of structure regulation and electrolyte optimization for achieving fast-charging performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300217-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn widespread concern due to the rich reserves, low price, and good sustainability of sodium, which are emerging as the alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) as the next-generation energy storage devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0001_R_d167095010e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Chem. Rev. Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Energy Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is challenging for SIBs to be satisfied with the requirements for high energy density and longer recharge mileage due to the commercial low-capacity hard carbon anode (i.e., &lt;300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0002_R_d167095010e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, metal sulfide materials, such as Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0003_R_d167095010e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Technol. Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CoS<sub><i>x</i></sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0004_R_d167095010e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Res. Chem. Eng. J. J. Energy Chem.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoS<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0005_R_d167095010e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Nano Rare Metals">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0006_R_d167095010e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SnS,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0007_R_d167095010e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and VS<sub><i>x</i></sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0008_R_d167095010e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Electrochim. Acta">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been widely developed for replacing the hard carbon anodes due to their higher capacities (i.e., &gt;400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), strong redox reversibility, and abundant resources. Among various metal sulfides, VS<sub>4</sub> and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are preferred as potential anode candidates for SIBs because of their abundant resource, high theoretical capacity (e.g., 1196 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for VS<sub>4</sub>, 625 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>), and the unique chain-like stacking structure. Nevertheless, VS<sub>4</sub> and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>-based materials always suffer from severe capacity decay and poor rate capability, which is caused by the drastic volumetric variation, sluggish ion transport kinetics, and sulfur shuttle effect during the sodiation/desodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0009_R_d167095010e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">9</a>, <a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0010_R_d167095010e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, structure design in diverse dimensions (e.g., nanograins,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0011_R_d167095010e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> nanorods,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0012_R_d167095010e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Nano Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> nanospheres,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0013_R_d167095010e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D hierarchical structured materials)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0014_R_d167095010e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> followed by carbon modification<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0015_R_d167095010e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> has been the most popular strategy to address the intrinsic issues of the metal sulfide anodes, which can promote the electrical conductivity and mitigate the volume change of metal sulfide anodes. A hierarchically structured Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/graphene aerogel composite was proposed for SIBs, demonstrating a stable capacity of 348 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 120 cycles at the current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0016_R_d167095010e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nanoscale">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yu et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0017_R_d167095010e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared the flower-like VS<sub>4</sub>@C anode, which achieved a stable capacity of 283.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 300 cycles at the current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. More recent studies demonstrate that constructing heterostructure in the bimetallic sulfides with different bandgaps can form built-in electric fields at the heterointerface, which can boost the sodium storage capability through the abundant active sites, and enhance electronic/ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0018_R_d167095010e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. J. Phys. Chem. Lett. Small Sci. Energy Mater. Adv.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, a stable capacity of 427.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 100 cycles at the current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the heterojunction-type Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> anode can be delivered, which can be attributed to the enhancements of reaction dynamics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0019_R_d167095010e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, electrolyte engineering is also an efficient approach to form a stabilized solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the electrode surface and then restrain the side reactions, giving rise to an improved performance of metal sulfide-based anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0020_R_d167095010e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Qin et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0021_R_d167095010e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed an ether-based electrolyte (i.e., 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether), in which a thin and stable SEI layer was formed on the electrode, suppressing the continuous electrolyte decomposition, and then endowing an excellent sodium storage capability (i.e., 341.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 250 cycles at the current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>). However, the cycle life and rate performances of these reported metal sulfide anodes are still unsatisfactory to meet the demands in practical application. Therefore, it is still necessary to rationally regulate structure/component and optimize electrolyte system to boost the performances of metal sulfide-based anodes.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we present a novel bimetallic sulfide wrapped by reduced graphene oxide (i.e., Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO) as the anode for SIBs via a one-pot hydrothermal strategy, in which the well-distributed heterostructures between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> can provide abundant active interfaces and phase boundaries to enhance the charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics. With an electrolyte optimization strategy, the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode demonstrates fascinating rate capability and long lifespan (i.e., 780 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 1000 cycles at the current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in the electrolyte of 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethyl ether (DME), which is better than those of most reported before. These results indicate that the rational designed architecture and the optimized electrolyte are vital to stabilize the electrode simultaneously.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300217-sec-0002-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0003-title">2.1 Synthetic Strategy</h3>
            
            <p>The synthetic procedure for Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO composite is illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, VO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and Bi<sup>3+</sup>, dissociated from NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> and Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, could be absorbed on the rGO surface through electronegativity and rich oxygen-containing functional groups, where the added thioacetamide (TAA) served as the sulfur source and reducing agent. Then, in the acidic solution environment (i.e., NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O + H<sup>+</sup>), TAA can be hydrolyzed (i.e., CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ CH<sub>3</sub>CONH<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>S), while V<sup>5+</sup> in VO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> can be reduced to V<sup>4+</sup> by H<sub>2</sub>S (i.e., H<sub>2</sub>S + 6VO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> + 28H<sup>+</sup> → 6V<sup>4+</sup> + SO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> + 15H<sub>2</sub>O) during the hydrothermal process. By this way, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods formed on the rGO substrate preferentially and then the VS<sub>4</sub> was coated on the surface of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Finally, the heterostructure Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO can be formed by magically self-assembly.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c9d28b8c-a0b5-4baf-95da-89b41e5565b7/sstr202300217-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c9d28b8c-a0b5-4baf-95da-89b41e5565b7/sstr202300217-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7838ea95-d0f4-469a-a03e-32c0afff9c1d/sstr202300217-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c9d28b8c-a0b5-4baf-95da-89b41e5565b7/sstr202300217-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0004-title">2.2 Architecture Features</h3>
            
            <p>The morphology and structural characteristics of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO are first characterized. It can be found that the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods and VS<sub>4</sub> nanospheres cross-linked and were wrapped by the rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0001">1b–d</a>), which is different from the stacked Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO composites (Figure S1 and S2, Supporting Information). In addition, the heterostructure of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO can be confirmed by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED), in which the concentric diffraction rings match the multi phases of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0001">1e</a>). Besides, the lattice spacing of 2.70 and 3.76 Å matching with the (410) and (211) lattice plane of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>, respectively, can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0001">1f</a>, further confirming the existence of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> heterojunction. The fine distribution of Bi, V, S, and C elements detected by EDXA mapping confirms the main components of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0001">1g</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The crystalline characteristics of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO were further identified by the X-ray powder diffraction (XRD), in which the diffraction peaks accord well with the different crystal planes in the orthorhombic Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS No. 84-0729) and monoclinic VS<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 72-1294), respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2a</a>). Two main characteristic peaks located at 1353 and 1593 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be observed in Raman spectroscopy, which agrees to the graphite <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> carbon (G-band) and disordered <i>sp</i><sup>3</sup> carbon/structural defects (D-band) of the rGO, respectively, confirming the existence of rGO with much defect sites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2b</a>). In addition, the characteristic peaks of Bi (orange square) and V (blue square) alternately can be observed by the elemental line scanning of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, which indicates the presence of two different phase in the composite, confirming the existence of heterostructure between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0022_R_d167095010e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the chemical compositions and valence of elements were further determined by X-ray photoelectron spectrometry (XPS) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2d,e</a>. Specifically speaking, the Bi (III) oxidation state was confirmed by the binding energy of Bi 4<i>f</i><sub>7/2</sub> (i.e., 158.6 eV) and Bi 4<i>f</i><sub>5/2</sub> (i.e., 163.9 eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2d</a>), while two main peaks at 525.0 and 517.6 eV in the high-resolution V 2<i>p</i> spectrogram match well to the 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> characteristic peaks of V (IV) oxidation state in the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0003_R_d167095010e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Technol. Energy Storage Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0008_R_d167095010e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Electrochim. Acta">8</a></sup></span> We find that a strong electron interaction is established between VS<sub>4</sub> and rGO through the V<span class="icomoon"></span>C bonds, which can be confirmed by the binding energy of V<span class="icomoon"></span>C bond at 521.7 and 514.2 eV in the V 2<i>p</i> spectrum. In addition, the existence of heterostructure between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> can be further judged by the opposite shift of V 2<i>p</i> and Bi 4<i>f</i> binding energy in the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0023_R_d167095010e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is because the electron cloud can flow from Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to VS<sub>4</sub> when the two materials with different band gaps are in contact, triggering the peak shifts of the V 2<i>p</i> and Bi 4<i>f</i> spectrum. This result can be confirmed by DFT calculation as shown in Figure S3, Supporting Information. Moreover, the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO possess an increased surface area of 13.52 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0002">2f</a>) compared to 7.49 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> (Figure S4, Supporting Information), which can be ascribed to the introduction of rGO.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/49f27689-de6b-41fb-9516-5afe536b275f/sstr202300217-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/49f27689-de6b-41fb-9516-5afe536b275f/sstr202300217-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6756dec0-cf9b-4feb-b005-6a8cba5fefb3/sstr202300217-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/49f27689-de6b-41fb-9516-5afe536b275f/sstr202300217-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

HR-XPS spectra of d) Bi 4<i>f</i> and e) V 2<i>p</i> in Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO, and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, respectively. f) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms and the corresponding pore size distribution of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0005-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The remarkable sodium storage capability of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO was first confirmed in a sodium-ion half-cell, in which the 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte was employed. The high reversibility and stability of sodium storage for Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO were confirmed by the measurement of cyclic voltammetry (CV) as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3a</a>, in which the CV curves can be overlapped well after the first cycle. Meanwhile, the cathodic peaks at 1.95 and 1.55 V represent the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the interlayer space of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> to form Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VS<sub>4</sub> (i.e., <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> + <i>x</i>e<sup>−</sup> ↔ Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + VS<sub>4</sub> +<i> x</i>e<sup>−</sup> ↔ Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VS<sub>4</sub>), respectively. The sharp cathodic peak located at 1.12 V should be attributed to the formation of SEI layer and the conversion reaction from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to the Bi<sup>0</sup>–Na<sub>2</sub>S composites (i.e., Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> + (6 − <i>x</i>)Na<sup>+</sup> + (6 − <i>x</i>)e<sup>−</sup> ↔ 2Bi<sup>0</sup> + 3Na<sub>2</sub>S). Subsequently, the multistep Na–Bi alloying reactions occur at 0.62 and 0.42 V (i.e., Bi<sup>0</sup> + <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + <i>x</i>e<sup>−</sup> ↔ Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi). And then followed the conversion reaction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VS<sub>4</sub> operates at ≈0.25 V (i.e., Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VS<sub>4</sub> + (8 − <i>x</i>)Na<sup>+</sup> + (8 − <i>x</i>)e<sup>−</sup> ↔ 4Na<sub>2</sub>S + V<sup>0</sup>). Afterward, during the first anodic scan, the two peaks located at 0.59 and 0.77 V represent reversible dealloying reactions from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi to Bi (i.e., Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Bi ↔ <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + Bi<sup>0</sup> + <i>x</i>e<sup>−</sup>), while the peaks at 1.87 and 2.10 V correspond to the reversible conversion reaction to rebuild Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> (i.e., 2Bi<sup>0</sup> + 3Na<sub>2</sub>S ↔ Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> + 6Na<sup>+</sup> + 6e<sup>−</sup>, V<sup>0</sup> + 4Na<sub>2</sub>S ↔ VS<sub>4</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>−</sup>). Note that in the following scans, the peak at 1.87 V is integrated to the peak at 2.10 V, which can be attributed to structure evolution in the electrode during the discharge/charge procedure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0024_R_d167095010e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fbef5220-0b8a-4392-91b7-60d25148d348/sstr202300217-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fbef5220-0b8a-4392-91b7-60d25148d348/sstr202300217-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/094b2de2-cda6-4222-8a2b-3622bcc9fc4b/sstr202300217-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fbef5220-0b8a-4392-91b7-60d25148d348/sstr202300217-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO. a) CV curves of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO. b) Comparative the initial (dis-)charge curves of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO, VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Long cycle performance and d) rate performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO. e) Comparison of rate performance with other reported anodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The typical (dis-)charge curves of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3b</a>, where the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO show a higher initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 81.7% than that of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO (i.e., 57.6%) and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO (i.e., 79.9%). The improved ICE of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO is attributed to that: 1) the formed heterointerface can improve the electron transfer between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub>, thereby inducing electron redistribution to form built-in electric fields and improve the electronic structure of the active centers to achieve a higher reversibility of the conversion reactions and 2) the rGO can improve the electrical conductivity of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> and lessen the contact between the metal sulfide and the electrolyte, giving rise to the mitigated decomposition of the electrolyte. Moreover, the long cycle performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO is demonstrated at the current density of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, where the high capacity of 746.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 1000 cycles was obtained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3c</a>). In stark contrast, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO, VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> cannot work anymore after less than 425 cycles at the current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3c</a> and S5, Supporting Information), which may be caused by the dissolution of polysulfides (i.e., NaPSs) and the followed shuttle effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0025_R_d167095010e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, another great feature of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode is the excellent rate capability, in which high capacities of 941.9, 893.3, 848.2, 808.6, and 778.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered after activation, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3d</a>). This result of rate capability is superior to those of most Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> or VS<sub>4</sub>-based anodes reported previously (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0003">3e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0026_R_d167095010e1455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nanoscale ChemSusChem Energy Storage Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J. Nano Res. J. Energy Chem. Energy Environ. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0006-title">2.4 Electrochemical Analysis</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The reasons for the outstanding performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO were first investigated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). It can be found that the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of the fresh Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode (i.e., 17.08 Ω) is much lower than 27.87 and 19.46 Ω of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4a</a> and Table S1, Supporting Information). This should be attributed to the formation of a stable SEI and the suppressed decomposition of electrolyte. Meanwhile, the rGO can improve the electrical conductivity of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> as well as maintain the electrode integrity. The dynamic process was further studied by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) with a pulse current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 10 min between 30 min rest intervals as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4b,c</a>. The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>+) was acquired via the following equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0027_R_d167095010e1514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="sstr202300217-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/48effc41-8e2a-47f4-b29b-3e0ceed95d8d/sstr202300217-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26884062:media:sstr202300217:sstr202300217-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/sstr202300217-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mo>=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>4</mn>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>L</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>π</mi>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo>(</mo>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mo>Δ</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>s</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mo>Δ</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>

                                       </mrow>

                                 </mrow>

                              </mrow>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$$D = \frac{4 L^{2}}{\pi \tau} \left(\right. \frac{\cdot E_{s}}{\cdot E_{t}} \left.\right)$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>L</i> is the sodium-ion diffusion length, <i>τ</i> is the relaxation time, and <i>t</i>, Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub>, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> are the duration of the current pulse, potential variation between steps, and voltage variation during the pulse process, respectively. It can be found that the calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><i>+</i> of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO in the sodiation processes is higher than those of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4d</a>). This should be attributed to the formed heterostructures between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> as well as the solid rGO skeleton with effective electronic conducting network, which can enhance the electrical conductivity and then boost the electron transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics.</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b6f8ad5-9495-4fe5-95c1-b28ab3a72876/sstr202300217-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b6f8ad5-9495-4fe5-95c1-b28ab3a72876/sstr202300217-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c5177e3-724f-4de6-bfe1-bdff60594fff/sstr202300217-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b6f8ad5-9495-4fe5-95c1-b28ab3a72876/sstr202300217-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical analysis. a) Nyquist plots of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO, and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode. b) GITT curves and c) voltage versus time curves of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO electrode for a single GITT during the discharge process. d) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO at different sodiation state. e) CV curves of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode at different scan rate. f) The corresponding linear relationship between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>. g) Contribution ratio of capacitive capacities at different scan rates.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The reasons for high capacity, especially at high current densities, can be further investigated by CV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4e</a>). Generally, a relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) in a scan voltammetry measurement can be calculated as follows
               <div class="inline-equation" id="sstr202300217-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7a50e61a-931d-475b-8c40-89eab628b3e5/sstr202300217-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26884062:media:sstr202300217:sstr202300217-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/sstr202300217-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo>=</mo>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$$i = a v^{b}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are regulable parameters. The <i>b</i> values for the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode at the peaks 1, 2, and 3 are 0.82, 1.0, and 0.92, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4f</a>), by plotting the log (<i>i</i>) and log (<i>v</i>). Thus, it can be confirmed that the pseudocapacitive process is mainly responsible for the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode based on the classification of <i>b</i>-value (i.e., 0.5 &lt; <i>b</i> &lt; 1). Then, the relative contribution of the pseudocapacitive process and the diffusion process can be quantitatively calculated by the following equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0028_R_d167095010e1719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="sstr202300217-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8609183a-b61e-45c2-b261-910cd92d3669/sstr202300217-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26884062:media:sstr202300217:sstr202300217-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/sstr202300217-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo>=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo stretchy="false">/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                              <mtext> </mtext>
                              <mtext> </mtext>
                              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mtext> and</mtext>
                              <mtext> </mtext>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mtext> </mtext>
                              <mtext>are</mtext>
                              <mtext> </mtext>
                              <mtext>empirical parameters)</mtext>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$$i = k_{1} v + k_{2} v^{1 / 2} \textrm{ } \textrm{ } \left(\right. k_{1} \text{ and} \textrm{ } k_{2} \textrm{ } \text{are} \textrm{ } \text{empirical parameters)}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be calculated from the slope of the <i>iv</i><sup>−1/2</sup> versus <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0004">4g</a>). According to the above analysis, we conclude that 1) the pseudocapacitive process dominated the sodium storage mechanism in Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, particularly at the high scans; 2) the pseudocapacitive contribution can boost the capacity markedly, which is responsible for the high capacities at high current densities; and 3) the pseudocapacitive contribution of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO (i.e., 99.12%) is the largest compared to those of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/rGO (i.e., 90%) and VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO (i.e., 98%, as shown in Figure S6 and S7, Supporting Information) at 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is consistent with the best rate capability of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO. The pseudocapacitive charge storage of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO can be attributed to the rich boundaries during the self-assembly process as well as the much defect sites of the rGO.</div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0007-title">2.5 Electrode Evolution and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To further figure out the reasons for the excellent electrochemical performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO, the structural evolution analysis was conducted by examining the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode after 200 cycles. First, the SEM images of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode before and after 200 cycles are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5a–d</a>. It is can be observed that the whole electrode is well preserved and the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were still closely anchored on the rGO during the cycling, indicating good contact between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub> and rGO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). Then, we find that the sizes of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO were reduced compared to the pristine (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5a,b</a>), which may be derived from the pulverization in the continuous discharge/charge process. This phenomenon is consistent with the observed trend of capacity increase during the long cycling, in which more active sites of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO are exposed, giving rise to an increased capacity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/90a78690-1913-4e51-958e-bc85c06833b5/sstr202300217-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/90a78690-1913-4e51-958e-bc85c06833b5/sstr202300217-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96b873fa-a0dc-4c6d-9c22-dd3e50d62969/sstr202300217-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/90a78690-1913-4e51-958e-bc85c06833b5/sstr202300217-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrode evolution. SEM, TEM images of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode a,b) before and c,d) after 200 cycles. e,f) HR-TEM images of the full discharged and charged state after 200 cycles. g) Schematic illustration of the structural evolution of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To better investigate the evolution of the crystalline phase, the HR-TEM of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO electrode at full discharged/charged state after 200 cycles was executed. We can find that some lattice fringe with the interlayer spacing of 2.18, 3.26, 2.19, and 2.73 Å can correspond well to the (111), (200), (111), and (110) planes of V<sup>0</sup>, Na<sub>2</sub>S, NaBi, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phases at the full discharged state, respectively. However, the interlayer spacing of 2.71, 3.15, and 2.44 Å can correspond well to the (301), (−202), and (004) planes of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phases at the full charged state, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5e,f</a>). When compared to the pristine structure, the heterostructures between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> and new phases formed heterostructures in the regenerated and recrystallized composites can be observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5e,f</a> and S8, Supporting Information), which can offer more active sites and abundant phase boundaries to store Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to the increased capacity and faster kinetics of the conversion reactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0005">5g</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="sstr202300217-sec-0008-title">2.6 Electrolyte Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Interestingly, we find that the electrochemical performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO can be significantly influenced by the electrolyte. In detail, when the solvent was changed from DME to ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate/fluoroethylene carbonate (EC/DMC/FEC), a fast capacity decay can be observed (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6a</a>), demonstrating the importance of the solvents. Thus, the solvent difference in the different electrolytes was analyzed by Raman spectrogram, in which the changed stretching vibrations of P–F vibration at 768.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be applied to comprehend the interaction between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> in the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6b</a>). Note that the peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> shows different redshift degrees when the NaPF<sub>6</sub> was dissolved by different solvents. It can be found that the largest redshift degree (i.e., 740.8 cm<sup>−1</sup>) can be observed in the 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte compared to that in the 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/FEC electrolyte (i.e., 741.8 cm<sup>−1</sup>). These comparative results indicate that the Na<sup>+</sup>–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interaction in DME-based electrolyte is weaker than that in the EC-based electrolyte. This should be attributed to the higher steric hindrance of the Na<sup>+</sup>[DME]<sub>2</sub> bidentate chelation when compared to that in that of the Na<sup>+</sup>[EC]<sub>4</sub> monodentate chelation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="sstr202300217-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6d542736-3fbe-4885-9b1b-d9c4698cfed5/sstr202300217-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6d542736-3fbe-4885-9b1b-d9c4698cfed5/sstr202300217-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d68c0f35-aaf5-40fe-9965-7335ed3e5ce2/sstr202300217-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6d542736-3fbe-4885-9b1b-d9c4698cfed5/sstr202300217-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=sstr202300217-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsstr.202300217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
Electrolyte analysis. a) Cycling performance of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode in the electrolyte of 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME and 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/FEC. b) Raman spectra of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in different kinds of solvents. c) Comparative interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and different solvents. Solvation structure and interfacial model of the electrolyte of d) 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME and e) 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/FEC. f) Calculated binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvents.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Based on the above analysis results, the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structures and corresponding interfacial models derived from the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation process on the surface of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anodes are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6d,e</a>, in which the Na<sup>+</sup>[solvent]<sub><i>x</i></sub>[anion] (i.e., <i>x</i>, calculated by the molar ratio of electrolyte compositions) was used to describe the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#sstr202300217-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="sstr202300217-bib-0029_R_d167095010e2112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the thermodynamic properties of the solvation coordination were analyzed to interpret the electrochemical stability of the electrolytes based on the lowest-unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy level. In the 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME electrolyte (i.e., Na<sup>+</sup>[DME]<sub>9.61</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>], Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6d</a>), the first solvation shell was composed of Na<sup>+</sup>–DME pairs, while the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion can keep farther from the surface of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode due to the stronger Na<sup>+</sup>–DME interaction (i.e., −1.38 eV vs −0.44 eV of Na<sup>+</sup>–EC, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6f</a>), leading to the improved stability of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. During the desolvation process, a stable SEI can be formed after the Na<sup>+</sup>–DME–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (i.e., −0.044 Hartree, Figure S9, Supporting Information) accept one electron, and then further suppress the electrolyte decomposition, the dissolution of NaPSs, and the followed shuttle effect. As a result, a superior long-term cycle of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode can be observed in the DME electrolyte.</p>
            
            <p>When the DME solvent was replaced by EC/DMC/FEC (i.e., Na<sup>+</sup>[EC]<sub>7.5</sub>[DMC]<sub>5.9</sub>[FEC]<sub>0.7</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>], Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6e</a>), the first solvation shell was mainly composed of Na<sup>+</sup>–EC (i.e., −0.44 eV), while the FEC additive can participate in regulating the solvation shell through the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and FEC (i.e., −0.37 eV, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#sstr202300217-fig-0006">6f</a>). PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> can appear more frequently on the surface of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode due to the stronger interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the Na<sup>+</sup>[EC]<sub>7.5</sub>[DMC]<sub>5.9</sub>[FEC]<sub>0.7</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>] electrolyte. As a result, when the anode accepts one electron, the Na<sup>+</sup>–FEC–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> could be preferentially reduced to form a SEI on the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode surface due to the low stability of Na<sup>+</sup>–FEC–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (i.e., −0.098 Hartree vs −0.073 Hartree of Na<sup>+</sup>–EC–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, Figure S9, Supporting Information). However, the formed SEI is insufficient to effectively mitigate the electrolyte decomposition because of the low LUMO energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>–EC–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. Thus, a fast capacity decay of the Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode is observed in the electrolyte of 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/FEC. All above results demonstrate that the electrolyte compatibility is of great significance in stabilizing the electrode performance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="sstr202300217-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300217-sec-0009-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a novel heterostructured Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO anode was successfully synthesized via a one-pot hydrothermal strategy, in which the heterojunction between Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and VS<sub>4</sub> can promote the charge transfer and improve the Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics by the formed internal electric field. Combined with electrolyte engineering, the as-prepared Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO demonstrates an excellent high-rate capability and long lifespan, which is superior to most of the metal sulfide-based anode reported previously. In addition, Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure and interfacial models are established to understand the relationship between electrolytes and electrode performance. All these confirm the significance of electrode material design and electrolyte optimization for improving the performance. We believe that the strategies in this work can be appropriate for synthesizing more electrode materials with greater fast-charging performance while improving the electrode performance from multidimensions.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="sstr202300217-sec-0010-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
